No Liability for Building a Bridge or Placing a Log When Victim Acts Deliberately
Ruling that deliberate act of victim cuts causal link

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on liability when a victim deliberately uses an unauthorized structure and explain why the intervening act of a free agent cuts causal connection.

Core Idea
A deliberate act of a free agent severs legal causation, so indirect cause bears no liability.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses whether a person who builds an unauthorized bridge or places a log is liable if someone deliberately uses it and dies. The Hanafi school rules there is no liability, based on the principle that the victim's deliberate choice as a free agent intervenes and severs the causal chain. Understanding this rule clarifies how Hanafi law balances indirect causation (tasabbub) and direct causation (mubasharah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is building a bridge without permission or placing a log in the road. The ruling: no liability on the builder/placer when the victim deliberately passes over and dies.
2
Locate the first proof
The first proof distinguishes between indirect cause (tasabbub) of the builder and direct cause (mubasharah) of the victim. When both meet, attribution goes to the direct cause.
The builder's act is taa'addi (transgression) but indirect.
3
Locate the second proof
The second proof states that the intervening deliberate act of a free agent (fa'il mukhtar) cuts the legal connection (nisbah). This is illustrated by the analogy of a digger and a thrower.
The analogy shows that the thrower's voluntary act severs the chain from the digger.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools may hold the builder partially liable for creating a hazard, but the Hanafi position is that the victim's free choice removes liability.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.
5
Apply the principle to similar cases
This ruling applies to any situation where a hazard is created and a competent adult deliberately chooses to encounter it, knowing the risk.
If the victim is compelled or unaware, liability may arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام کی اجازت کے بغیر پل بنایا پس عمد ا اس پر کوئی گذرا اور ہلاک ہو گیا تو ضمان پل بنانے والے پر نہیں ، اسی طرح راستہ میں شہتیر رکھی اور ایک بقیہ راستہ پر چلنے کی جگہ ہونے کے باوجود اس شہتیر پر چل کر گر پڑا تو ضامن کون ہوگا
قال ومن جعل قنطرة بغير إذن الإمام فتعمد رجل المرور عليها فعطب فلاضمان على الذي جعل قنطرة وكذالك إن وضع خشبة في الطريق فتعمد رجل المرور عليها لأن الأول تعد هو تسبب والثانى تعد هو مباشرة فكان الإضافة إلى المباشر أولى ولأن تخلل فعل فاعل مختار يقطع النسبة كما في الحافر مع الملقي.
ترجمہ امام گندہ نے فرمایا اور جس نے امام کی اجازت کے بغیر تیل بنایا پس عمداً اس کے اوپر کوئی شخص گذرا پس وہ ہلاک ہو گیا تو پل بنانے والے پر کوئی مہمان نہیں ہے اور اسی طرح اگر راستہ میں لکڑی رکھی پس اس کے اوپر کوئی شخص عمدا گذرا اس لئے کہ اول ایسی تعدی ہے کہ وہ جیب ہے اور ثانی ایسی تعدی ہے کہ وہ مباشرت ہے تو مباشر کی طرف اضافت اولی ہوگی اور اس لئے کہ فاعل مختار کے فعل کا درمیان میں آنا نسبت کو منقطع کر دیتا ہے، جیسے حافر میں ملقن کے ساتھ ۔ تشریح ایک شخص نے امام کی اجازت کے بغیر پل بنا دیا اب خالد اس کے اوپر کو قصدا گذرا حالانکہ وہ ایسا ہے کہ دوسری جگہ کو بھی گذر سکتا ہے مگر وہ اس کے اوپر کو گذرا اور وہ پل ٹوٹ گیا جس سے خالہ ہو گیا تو پل بنانے والے پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا، اسی طرح اگر زید نے
He built a bridge without the Imam's permission, then someone deliberately passed over it and died. There is no liability on the one who built the bridge. Similarly, if he placed a log in the road, and someone deliberately passed over it, ... because the first transgression is indirect, and the second transgression is direct, so attribution to the direct is more appropriate, and because the intervening act of a free agent cuts the connection, as in the case of the digger with the one who throws. Translation: Imam [Ghundah] said: And whoever built a bridge without the Imam's permission, and a man deliberately passed over it and perished, there is no liability on the one who built the bridge. Similarly, if he placed a log in the road, and a man deliberately passed over it, because the first transgression is indirect and the second is direct, so attribution to the direct is more appropriate, and because the intervening act of a free agent cuts the connection, as in the digger with the thrower. Explanation: A person built a bridge without the Imam's permission. Khalid deliberately passed over it, even though he could have passed elsewhere, and the bridge broke and he died. There is no liability on the bridge builder. Similarly, if Zaid...
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof addressing cases where someone builds a bridge (qantara) or places a log (khashaba) without authorization, and another person deliberately uses that obstacle and dies. The ruling states that the builder/placer is not liable, even though their act was unauthorized (transgression). The proof is twofold. First, the victim's action is a direct cause (mubasharah) of his own death, while the builder's action is only an indirect cause (tasabbub). In Hanafi law, when direct and indirect causes meet, the direct cause takes precedence for attribution. Second, and more importantly, the deliberate act of a free, competent agent (fa'il mukhtar) intervenes and cuts the legal causal connection (nusbah) between the original act and the death. This is likened to the case of a digger and a thrower: if a man digs a well (unlawfully) and another person intentionally throws someone into it, the thrower is liable, not the digger, because the thrower's voluntary act severs the chain. The commentary illustrates this with an example of a man who deliberately chooses to walk over a makeshift bridge despite an alternative path, absolving the builder.

Key Terms
قنطرة (qanṭarah): A bridge or archway built across a road or water.
تسبب (tasabbub): Indirect causation, where the act is remote and depends on another's choice.
فاعل مختار (fā‘il mukhtār): A free and competent agent who acts with choice; such an act interrupts legal attribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the bridge builder not liable when a person deliberately passes over it and dies?
A) Because the bridge was built without permission
B) Because the victim's deliberate act severs the causal connection
C) Because the builder did not intend harm
D) Because the bridge was safe

Key Takeaway
When a victim deliberately chooses to use an unauthorized structure, the builder's indirect causation is overridden, and no liability arises.

No Liability for Builder if Victim Intentionally Passes Over Unauthorized Bridge
Ruling with reasoning on causation when victim intentionally uses dangerous structure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi causation rule that a direct act (mubāshara) overrides an indirect cause (sabab) when the victim is a free agent (fā'il mukhtār), and state the ruling on liability for unauthorized structures when the victim intentionally uses them.

Core Idea
A builder is not liable if the victim intentionally uses his unauthorized structure — the victim's direct act breaks the causal chain.

This lesson examines a key causation principle in Hanafi tort law: when both the tortfeasor and the victim act wrongfully, but the victim's act is direct and intentional, liability shifts. The text from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani uses the example of an unauthorized bridge and a plank placed in a road to illustrate that the indirect cause (building/placing) is overridden by the victim's direct act of intentionally crossing. Understanding this rule clarifies when a tortfeasor escapes liability due to the victim's own deliberate conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being judged (building a bridge without permission) and what the ruling is (no liability if victim intentionally passes). Note that the ruling is the same for placing a plank.
The ruling is a negative liability: damān not imposed.
2
Distinguish the two causation levels
Classify the builder's act as indirect causation (sabab) and the victim's act as direct causation (mubāshara). Recognize that in Hanafi law, direct causation takes precedence over indirect when both exist.
The text explicitly says 'al-tānī tā'addī huwa mubāshara' – the second transgression is direct.
3
Understand the principle of intervening free agent
Analyze the analogical reasoning: the intervention of a free agent (fā'il mukhtār) cuts off the causal link between an earlier cause and the harm. The text analogizes to the case of a digger and a thrower (al-hāfir ma'a al-mulqī).
This principle is a cornerstone of Hanafi causation theory in torts.
4
Apply the reasoning to the plank scenario
Confirm that the same logic applies: placing a plank in the road is an indirect cause; the victim's intentional stepping on it is direct. Hence the placer is not liable.
The commentary emphasizes that the victim had an alternative path, reinforcing free choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن جعل قنطرة بغير إذن الإمام فتعمد رجل المرور عليها فعطب فلاضمان على الذي جعل قنطرة وكذالك إن وضع خشبة في الطريق فتعمد رجل المرور عليها لأن الأول تعد هو تسبب والثانى تعد هو مباشرة فكان الإضافة إلى المباشر أولى ولأن تخلل فعل فاعل مختار يقطع النسبة كما في الحافر مع الملقي.
ترجمہ امام گندہ نے فرمایا اور جس نے امام کی اجازت کے بغیر پل بنایا پس عمداً اس کے اوپر کوئی شخص گذرا پس وہ ہلاک ہو گیا تو پل بنانے والے پر کوئی مہمان نہیں ہے اور اسی طرح اگر راستہ میں لکڑی رکھی پس اس کے اوپر کوئی شخص عمدا گذرا اس لئے کہ اول ایسی تعدی ہے کہ وہ جیب ہے اور ثانی ایسی تعدی ہے کہ وہ مباشرت ہے تو مباشر کی طرف اضافت اولی ہوگی اور اس لئے کہ فاعل مختار کے فعل کا درمیان میں آنا نسبت کو منقطع کر دیتا ہے، جیسے حافر میں ملقن کے ساتھ ۔ تشریح ایک شخص نے امام کی اجازت کے بغیر پل بنا دیا اب خالد اس کے اوپر کو قصدا گذرا حالانکہ وہ ایسا ہے کہ دوسری جگہ کو بھی گذر سکتا ہے مگر وہ اس کے اوپر کو گذرا اور وہ پل ٹوٹ گیا جس سے خالہ ہو گیا تو پل بنانے والے پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا، اسی طرح اگر زید نے راستہ میں لکڑی ڈالی مگر سارے راستہ کو اس نے نہیں گھیرا بلکہ راستہ کا کچھ حصہ خالی پڑا ہے مگر خالد قصد الکڑی کے اوپر کو چلا اور پھسل کر گزر مر گیا تو زید پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ دونوں صورتوں میں تعدی دونوں کی ہے زید کی بھی اور خالد کی بھی مگر زید کا فعل سبب ہے اور خالد کا فعل مباشرت ہے اور مباشرت کو سب پر ترجیح ہوا کرتی ہے اس لئے زید پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا ۔ نیز اگر زید نے کنواں کھودا اور بکر نے خالد کو پکڑ کر اس میں گراد یا نمان بکر پر آئیگا زید پر نہیں کیونکہ کنواں کھودنا سبب ہے اور بکر کا فعل فاعل مختار کا فعل ہے اور سبب کے بعد جب فاعل مختار کا فعل درمیان میں آجائے تو حکم کی اضافت فاعل مختار کی طرف ہو گی سبب کی جانب نہ ہوگی۔ یہ مسلم اصول ہے۔ ( بیناو فی درس الحسامی)
اس طرح یہاں زید کا فعل مذکور سبب ہے اور درمیان میں خالد کا فعل حائل ہے جو فاعل مختار ہے اس لئے اب حکم کی اضافت زید کی طرف نہ ہوگی۔
He (Imam Muhammad) said: Whoever builds a bridge without the Imam's permission, and a man intentionally passes over it and is killed, there is no liability on the one who built the bridge. Likewise, if he places a plank in the road and a man intentionally passes over it. Because the first transgression is indirect (tasabbub) and the second transgression is direct (mubāshara), so attribution to the direct actor is more appropriate. And because the intervention of a free agent's act cuts off the causal connection, as in the case of the digger and the thrower (al-hāfir ma'a al-mulqī). Translation: Imam (Muhammad) said: Whoever builds a bridge without the Imam's permission, then a man intentionally passes over it and is killed, there is no liability on the bridge-builder. Likewise, if he places a plank in the road and a man intentionally passes over it. Because the first transgression is indirect and the second is direct, so attribution to the direct actor is more appropriate. And because the intervention of a free agent's act cuts off the causal connection, as in the case of the digger and the thrower. Commentary: A person built a bridge without the Imam's permission. Khalid intentionally passed over it, even though he could have passed elsewhere, and the bridge collapsed killing him. The builder is not liable. Similarly, if Zayd placed a plank in the road but left part of the road open, and Khalid intentionally walked on the plank and slipped and died, Zayd is not liable. Because in both cases, both parties are transgressors, but Zayd's act is indirect and Khalid's is direct, and direct acts take precedence, so liability is not on Zayd. Also, if Zayd dug a well and Bakr grabbed Khalid and threw him into it, liability is on Bakr, not Zayd, because digging is indirect and Bakr's act is that of a free agent, and when a free agent's act intervenes after a cause, the ruling is attributed to the free agent, not the cause. This is an established principle. (Mentioned in Dars al-Husāmi) Similarly, here Zayd's mentioned act is a cause, and Khalid's intervening act is that of a free agent, so the ruling is not attributed to Zayd.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani concerning liability for death caused by a dangerous structure when the victim intentionally uses it. The text states that if someone builds a bridge without official permission (i.e., without the Imam's authorization), and a person intentionally passes over it and dies, the builder is not liable. The same applies if someone places a plank in a public road and a person intentionally steps on it and dies. The reasoning given is clear: the builder's act is indirect (tasabbub) while the victim's act is direct (mubāshara), and in Hanafi law, direct causation takes precedence over indirect causation when both are present. Furthermore, the intervention of a free agent (fā'il mukhtār) breaks the causal chain. The commentary expands with examples: if a builder constructs a bridge and a passerby intentionally chooses to cross it despite an alternative route, the builder is not liable. Similarly, if a plank is placed in a road but space remains, and the victim deliberately steps on the plank, the placer is not liable. The principle is further illustrated with the case of a well: if Zayd digs a well and Bakr throws Khalid into it, Bakr bears liability because his act is direct and he is a free agent. This is a fundamental rule in Hanafi tort law: an indirect cause (like digging a well) is overridden by a direct act (like throwing) if the direct actor is a free agent. The final sentence reiterates that in the bridge/plank scenario, Zayd's act remains a cause, but since Khalid's intentional act intervenes, Zayd is not liable.

Key Terms
قنطرة (qanṭara): A bridge or arch constructed over a road or stream.
سبب (sabab): An indirect cause that does not directly bring about the harm, such as digging a well or placing an object.
مباشرة (mubāshara): A direct act that immediately causes the harm, such as throwing someone or walking onto a dangerous structure.
فاعل مختار (fā'il mukhtār): A person acting with free will and choice, capable of being held liable for their actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if Zayd builds a bridge without permission and Khalid intentionally crosses it and dies, who is liable?
A) Zayd, because he built the bridge without permission.
B) Khalid, because he intentionally crossed it.
C) Both are equally liable.
D) Neither is liable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an indirect cause (sabab) does not incur liability if a free agent (fā'il mukhtār) intentionally and directly causes the harm through his own act.

Liability for Carrying vs. Wearing: Direct Control vs. Incidental Attire
Ruling on liability for objects in the road.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for death caused by a carried object versus a worn garment, and apply the distinction between direct control and incidental attire.

Core Idea
The carrier is liable because he directly controls the object; the wearer is not because wearing is not a hazardous act.

A person carrying a load in a public road is responsible for its safe passage; if it falls and causes death, he must pay blood money. But if a person is merely wearing a cloak and it slips off, he is not liable, even if it causes death. This ruling hinges on the difference between actively controlling an object and simply being clothed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two distinct scenarios: (1) a person carries an object that falls and kills someone, and (2) a person wears a cloak that falls and kills someone. Note that the second scenario includes both direct impact and tripping.
Both scenarios involve death caused by an object, but the ruling differs.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For the carrier: he is liable. For the wearer: he is not liable. This is the explicit ruling from Imam Muhammad.
Liability means paying diyah (blood money) to the heirs of the deceased.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The key distinction is direct control. A carrier actively holds and transports the object; its fall is directly attributable to his action. A wearer's cloak is part of his attire; its fall is an accident not linked to a voluntary act of control.
The Hanafi principle: liability follows from direct causation (ta'di).
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
Imagine a person carrying a bag and it falls, versus a person wearing a scarf that falls. The same distinction applies. Also consider if the wearer deliberately throws the cloak – then liability would attach because it becomes a direct act.
The exception proves the rule: if the wearer intentionally disposes the garment, the ruling changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
راستہ میں کسی چیز کو اٹھایا وہ کسی انسان پر گر گئی اور جس پر گری وہ ہلاک ہو گیا ، اٹھانے والا ضامن ہے اسی طرح وہ سامان عام راستہ پر گر پڑا کوئی ٹھوکر کھا کر گر پڑا ضامن کون ہوگا، اسی طرح کوئی شخص چادر اوڑھے ہوئے تھا کہ اچانک چادر گر پڑی اور پیچھے آنے والا چادر سے الجھ کر مر گیا ضامن کون ہوگا
قال ومن حمل شيئا فى الطريق فسقط على إنسان فعطب به إنسان فهو ضامن وكذا إذا سقط فتعثر به إنسان وإن كان رداء قد لبسه فسقط فعطب به إنسان لم يضمن وهذا اللفظ يشتمل الوجهين ترجمہ محمد نے فرمایا اور جس راستہ میں کسی چیز کو اٹھایا پس وہ کسی انسان پر گر گئی ، پس اس کی وجہ سے کوئی انسان ہلاک ہو گیا پس وہ ضامن ہے اور ایسے ہی جب کہ وہ سامان گر جائے پس اس میں انسان اچھے گیا اور وہ چادر ہو جس کو وہ پہنے ہوئے ہے وہ گر گئی پس اس کی وجہ سے کوئی انسان ہلاک ہو گیا تو وہ ضامن نہ ہوگا ، اور یہ لفظ دونوں صورتوں کو مشتمل ہے۔
تشریح راستہ میں کوئی شخص اپنا سامان لئے ہوئے جار ہا ہے وہ گر گیا جس سے کوئی آدمی مر گیا یا سامان گر گیا اور اس میں کوئی پھسل کرمر گیا تو ان دونوں صورتوں میں حامل پر ضمان واجب ہوگا۔ اور اگر چادر اوڑھے جا رہا ہے اور چادر گر گئی جس کی وجہ سے کوئی شخص مر گیا یا چادر گرگئی اور اس میں کوئی پھسل کر مر گیا تو ان دونوں صورتوں میں لا بس پر ضمان نہ ہوگا۔ تنبيه وهذا اللفظ يعني دوسری صورت میں جو فعطب یہ ہے اس میں عموم ہے کہ چادر کے گرنے سے مرے یا چادر میں پھسلنے سے دونوں کا حکم ایک ہے، اس پر عنایہ میں اعتراض کیا گیا ہے مگر اعتراض علیل ہے کیونکہ چادر گرنے سے ردیت کے مفقود ہونے کی وجہ سے پھسل کر مر نا ممکن ہے تو اس کو غیر متصور کہنا عجیب سی بات ہے۔
He carried something in the road and it fell on a person and that person died, the carrier is liable. Similarly, if the load fell and a person tripped over it and died, who is liable? Similarly, a person was wearing a cloak and suddenly the cloak fell and someone behind became entangled in it and died, who is liable? He (Imam Muhammad) said: Whoever carries something in the road and it falls on a person, causing his death, he is liable. Likewise, if it falls and a person trips over it and dies, he is liable. But if it is a cloak that he was wearing and it falls and causes death, he is not liable. This wording covers both aspects. Translation: Muhammad said: Whoever carries something in the road and it falls on a person, causing his death, he is liable. Likewise, if the load falls and a person trips over it and dies, he is liable. But if it is a cloak that he was wearing and it falls and causes death, he is not liable. This wording covers both aspects. Commentary: A person is carrying his load in the road. It falls and kills someone, or the load falls and someone slips on it and dies; in both cases the carrier is liable. But if he is walking wearing a cloak and the cloak falls and kills someone, or the cloak falls and someone slips on it and dies, in both cases the wearer is not liable. Note: The wording 'fa'aṭaba' (causes death) in the second case is general: whether death occurs from the fall of the cloak or from slipping on it, the ruling is the same. Some (like 'Ināyah) objected, but the objection is weak, because if the cloak falls, the loss of moisture on the ground can cause slipping, so it is possible; deeming it inconceivable is strange.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani concerning liability for death caused by objects in a public road. The text distinguishes between two scenarios. First, if a person is carrying an object (like a load) and it falls, either directly on a person or on the ground causing someone to trip and die, the carrier is liable. Second, if a person is wearing a cloak (rḍā') and it falls, causing death either by direct impact or by someone tripping over it, the wearer is not liable. The reasoning lies in the nature of possession and control: a carried object is under the carrier's direct control and he is responsible for its safe transport; a worn garment is considered part of one's attire and its accidental slip is not deemed a culpable act. The commentary reinforces this: the carrier (ḥāmil) is liable for his load, but the wearer (lābis) is not liable for his cloak. A note addresses a potential objection from the commentary 'Ināyah, which questioned whether slipping on a fallen cloak is possible. The author of Ashraf al-Hidāyah dismisses this objection, arguing that a fallen cloak can make the ground slippery (due to moisture or fabric), so it is plausible.

Key Terms
حامل (ḥāmil): A person carrying a load or object in the road, under whose control the load is.
لابس (lābis): A person wearing a garment (such as a cloak) that falls unexpectedly.
رداء (ridā'): A cloak or upper garment worn over the shoulders.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if someone is wearing a cloak and it falls, causing a person to trip and die, is the wearer liable?
A) Yes, because the cloak caused the death.
B) Yes, because the wearer should have secured the cloak.
C) No, because the wearer is not liable for a garment he is wearing.
D) No, only if the cloak fell directly on the victim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes liability based on direct control: a carrier is liable for a falling load, but a wearer is not liable for a falling garment.

Liability of Carrier vs Wearer for Objects Causing Death in the Path
Distinguishes liability for object placed vs worn in public path causing death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a carrier (ḥāmil) and a wearer (lābīs) in Hanafi law regarding liability for objects causing death in a public path, and apply the exception for unusual garments.

Core Idea
Carriers pay for harm; wearers do not, unless the garment is abnormal.

In Hanafi fiqh, not every accidental death caused by one's property leads to liability. This passage from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani clarifies a key distinction: a person carrying a load (ḥāmil) is liable if it falls and causes death, but a person simply wearing a garment (lābīs) is not. The reasoning hinges on the intent to preserve the object. An exception exists for wearing something unusual, where the wearer becomes like a carrier. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its legal cause ('illa).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the first part of the passage: the carrier's load falls and kills someone, or someone trips over it. Then, the second scenario: a garment being worn falls and kills someone. Note that the ruling differs.
2
Extract the ruling for each scenario
State: The carrier is liable (ḍāmin), the wearer is not liable. Write down the legal consequence: payment of diyah (blood money) vs. no payment.
3
Analyze the legal cause ('illa)
The passage gives the reason: the carrier intends to preserve his load, so a condition of safety is reasonable. The wearer does not intend to preserve his garment; imposing safety would cause hardship. Identify this as the 'illa (effective cause) for the distinction.
The 'illa is the presence or absence of intent to preserve.
4
Apply the 'illa to the exception
Imam Muhammad adds: if someone wears something not normally worn (e.g., a sack or hide), there is no legitimate need, so the 'illa of intent to preserve reappears. Thus, he is like a carrier and is liable.
The exception reinforces the 'illa: unusual wear = intent to preserve.
5
Confirm the muftā bihī position
The relied-upon ruling is: carrier liable, wearer not liable, except for unusual garments where the wearer is liable. This is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
Later commentaries affirm this as the correct position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن حمل شيئا فى الطريق فسقط على إنسان فعطب به إنسان فهو ضامن وكذا إذا سقط فتعثر به إنسان وإن كان رداء قد لبسه فسقط فعطب به إنسان لم يضمن وهذا اللفظ يشتمل الوجهين
والفرق أن حامل الشيء قاصد حفظه فلا حرج فى التقييد بوصف السلامة واللابس لا يقصد حفظ ما يلبسه فيخرج بالتقييد بما ذكرناه فجعلناه مباحا مطلقا وعن محمد أنه إذا لبس ما لا يلبس فهو كالحامل لأن الحاجة لاتدعو إلى لبسه.
He (Muhammad) said: And whoever carries something in the road and it falls on a person and causes that person's death, he is liable. Likewise, if it falls and a person trips over it. But if it is a garment that he was wearing and it falls and causes a person's death, he is not liable. And these words encompass both aspects. The difference is that the carrier intends to preserve his load, so there is no hardship in restricting it to the condition of safety. The wearer does not intend to preserve what he wears, so restricting it as we have mentioned would cause hardship. Thus we have made it absolutely permissible. And from Muhammad: If he wears something that is not normally worn, he is like the carrier, because there is no need for wearing it.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its legal reasoning (talil). It begins with a statement from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani outlining two scenarios. First, if a person carries an object in a public path and it falls and kills someone, or if someone trips over it and dies, the carrier is liable ( ضامن ). This is because the carrier is engaged in a deliberate act of transporting goods, and he is responsible for ensuring they do not cause harm. Second, if a person is simply wearing a garment (such as a cloak) and it falls off and causes death, he is not liable. The reason is that the wearer does not have the intention of safeguarding the garment; wearing is a normal activity and tying it to a condition of safety would cause undue hardship. Imam Muhammad adds an exception: if someone wears something that is not customarily worn (e.g., a sack or animal hide), he is considered like a carrier because there is no legitimate need, so he is liable. The earlier examples in the Urdu commentary (such as the case of a log partially blocking the road, or a well dug and someone thrown in) illustrate the broader principle that direct action (mubasharah) takes precedence over causation (sabab), but here the focus is on the carrier's intent. The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
حامل (ḥāmil): A person carrying an object (like goods or a load) in a public path.
لابس (lābīs): A person wearing a garment (e.g., a cloak) that is being worn normally.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability, specifically financial liability such as payment of blood money (diyah) for causing death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a carrier (ḥāmil) liable for harm caused by his load while a wearer (lābīs) is not?
A) The carrier is always more careful than the wearer.
B) The carrier intends to preserve his load, so safety conditions are reasonable; the wearer does not intend to preserve his garment.
C) The carrier is obligated to keep his load off the ground; the wearer is not.
D) The carrier's load is heavier and more dangerous.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability for objects causing death in a public path depends on the intent to preserve the object: carriers are liable, wearers are not, unless the garment is unusual.

Liability for Hanging a Lamp in a Clan Mosque
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for objects in a clan mosque

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on liability for objects placed in a clan mosque and explain the reasoning based on the nature of the act as worship.

Core Idea
Liability turns on whether the actor is a clan member and whether the act is worship.

When a person places a lamp, mat, or pebble in a mosque belonging to a specific clan, and that object causes a death, who bears the blood money? The answer depends on who placed it and on a deeper question: does the act of facilitating worship override ownership rights of the clan? This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute recorded by Imam Muhammad, where Abu Hanifa differentiates between clan members and outsiders, while the Sahibayn hold that acts of worship are open to all, making everyone immune from liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling categories
The passage presents three scenarios: (a) a clan member places the object, (b) a non-member places it, (c) either case under the Sahibayn's view. Determine the ruling for each: liable or not liable.
Liability here means paying blood money (diyah) if the object causes death.
2
Locate the opinions
Imam Muhammad first states the ruling: no liability for clan member, liability for non-member. Then the commentators specify this is Abu Hanifa's view. Then they report the Sahibayn's view: no liability in both cases.
The passage uses 'they said' (qālū) to attribute the first ruling to Abu Hanifa.
3
Analyze the proofs (istidlal)
Abu Hanifa's implied proof: the mosque is owned by the clan, so non-members are like trespassers; their placement is unauthorized hence they are liable. The Sahibayn's proof: these acts (hanging a lamp, placing mats) are acts of worship (qurubāt); everyone is permitted to perform them, so they cannot be restricted by a safety condition. They draw an analogy: if done with permission of one trustee, it is non-liable; similarly, since everyone is permitted, it is as if permission is general.
The argument is talil (reasoning by effective cause): the effective cause for non-liability is the permissibility of the act.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a non-member hangs a lamp in a clan mosque and it kills someone, Abu Hanifa says he pays blood money; the Sahibayn say he does not. This affects the responsibility of visitors and volunteers in mosques.
Later Hanafi fatwa prefers the Sahibayn view to encourage acts of worship.
5
Note the later tarjih
This passage does not explicitly choose a position, but later Hanafi texts (e.g., commentaries on al-Hidayah) typically adopt the Sahibayn view as mufta bihi, because it is more facilitative for worship.
This illustrates how the school's operative ruling may develop over time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وإذا كان المسجد للعشيرة فعلق رجل منهم فيه قنديلا أو جعل فيه بوارى أو حصاه فعطب به رجل لم يضمن وإن كان الذي فعل ذالك من غير العشيرة ضمن قالوا هذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا يضمن في الوجهين لأن هذه من القرب وكل أحد مأذون في إقامتها فلايتقيد بشرط السلامة كما إذا فعله بإذن واحد من أهل المسجد
He (Muhammad) said: And if the mosque belongs to a clan (ʿashīrah), and a man from among them hangs a lamp (qindīl) in it, or places mats (bawārī) or pebbles (ḥaṣāh), and a person is killed by it, he is not liable. But if the one who did that is not from the clan, he is liable. They (the mashāʾikh) said: This is according to Abū Ḥanīfah. And the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) said: he is not liable in both cases, because these are acts of worship (qurubāt), and everyone is permitted to perform them; so they are not restricted to the condition of safety, just as if he did it with the permission of one of the people of the mosque.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability for death caused by an object (e.g., a lamp, mat, or pebble) placed in a mosque belonging to a specific clan. The passage begins with a statement from Imam Muhammad: if a member of the clan places such an item and it causes death, he is not liable; but if a non-member does so, he is liable. The commentators (mashāʾikh) state that this is the position of Imam Abū Ḥanīfah. However, the two companions (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad al-Shaybānī) hold that in both cases there is no liability. Their reasoning: these are acts of worship (e.g., lighting a mosque, providing mats), and every person is permitted to do them for the sake of worship. Therefore, they are not restricted to the condition of safety—otherwise it would discourage acts of devotion. They analogize it to a case where someone does the same with the explicit permission of one of the mosque's trustees. The dispute hinges on whether the permissibility to perform such acts is general (everyone) or restricted to the clan (or those with permission). Abū Ḥanīfah gives weight to the clan's ownership, while the Ṣāḥibayn prioritize the act's nature as worship. The muftā bihī position is not explicitly stated in this passage, but later Hanafi works tend to follow the Ṣāḥibayn because it facilitates acts of worship.

Key Terms
عشيرة (ʿashīrah): A clan or tribe that owns a specific mosque.
قنديل (qindīl): A lamp or lantern hung in a mosque.
قربة (qurbah): An act of worship or drawing closer to Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), why is a non-member not liable for death caused by a lamp he hangs in a clan mosque?
A) Because the non-member had permission from the clan.
B) Because hanging a lamp is an act of worship, and everyone is permitted to perform acts of worship.
C) Because the lamp belongs to the mosque.
D) Because the death was accidental.

Key Takeaway
Liability for objects placed in a clan mosque depends on whether the actor is a clan member (Abu Hanifa) or whether the act is a qurbah (Sahibayn); the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view.

Distinction Between Carrier and Wearer in Liability
Explains why carrier is liable but wearer is not for damages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a carrier versus a wearer, identify the rationale based on intent and custom, and apply the exception for unusual items as per Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
A carrier intends preservation so faces liability; a wearer does not, so no liability—except for unusual attire.

In the Hanafi school, not every harmful action leads to liability. This passage explains why a person carrying an object is liable for damages, while a person wearing a garment is not. The key is intent and custom: the carrier intends to preserve the item, so a condition of safety is reasonable; the wearer has no such intent, so imposing a safety condition would cause hardship. An exception arises when the worn item is not customary, per Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties
Recognize the carrier (ḥāmil) who intends preservation and the wearer (lābis) who does not.
2
Understand the rationale
The carrier’s intent makes it reasonable to hold him to a condition of safety; the wearer lacks that intent, so a condition would cause hardship.
3
Derive the default ruling
Carrier is liable if safety condition breached; wearer is absolutely not liable for damages caused by the worn item.
This is based on the principle of removing hardship (rafʿ al-ḥaraj).
4
Note the exception from Imam Muhammad
If someone wears an item not customarily worn (e.g., a saddle or load), he is treated as a carrier and liable, because there is no genuine need.
This shows the role of custom (ʿurf) in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply to a scenario
E.g., a person carrying a package drops it and causes injury: liable. A person wearing a cloak that tears and falls: not liable. But if the cloak is an unusual item like a heavy blanket, then liable per Muhammad’s view.
The exception prevents misuse of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق أن حامل الشيء قاصد حفظه فلا حرج فى التقييد بوصف السلامة واللابس لا يقصد حفظ ما يلبسه فيخرج بالتقييد بما ذكرناه فجعلناه مباحا مطلقا وعن محمد أنه إذا لبس ما لا يلبس فهو كالحامل لأن الحاجة لاتدعو إلى لبسه.
ترجمہ اور فرق یہ ہے کہ کسی پیر کو اٹھانے والا اس کی حفاظت کا ارادہ کرتا ہے تو وصف سلامتی کے ساتھ مقید کرنے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اور پہننے والا جس کپڑے کو پہنتا ہے اس کی حفاظت کا ارادہ نہیں کرتا پس اس وصف کے ساتھ مقید کرنے میں جس کا ہم نے ذکر کیا ہے تنگی لازم آئے گی۔ تو ہم نے اس کو مطلقا مباح قرار دیا اور محمد سے منقول ہے کہ جب ایسی چیز پہنی جو پہنی نہیں جاتی (عادةُ ) تو وہ حامل کے مثل ہے اس لئے کہ حاجت اس کے پہننے کی طرف داعی نہیں ہے۔
تشریح اٹھانے اور پہننے میں فرق کیوں کیا گیا کہ اول میں ضمان واجب ہے اور ثانی میں نہیں ہے۔ تو اب اس کو بیان فرماتے ہیں کہ حامل کا ارادہ سامان کی حفاظت کا ہوتا ہے تو اگر یہاں یہ قید لگا دی جائے کہ وصف سلامتی ضروری ہے ورنہ ضمان لازم ہوگا تو اس میں کوئی حرج اور تنگی نہیں ہے اور کپڑے پہنے والے کا مقصد کپڑے کی حفاظت نہیں ہے تو اگر یہاں بھی وصف سلامتی کی قید لگادی جائے تو جرج کثیر لازم آئے گا۔ اس لئے کپڑے پہنے کو مطلقاً جائز قرار دیا گیا بغیر کسی قید کے لہذا لا بس پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ امام محمد سے منقول ہے کہ اگر کسی نے ایسی چیز پہنی جو عاد پہنی نہیں جاتی جیسے جھول ، گدھے کی گون وغیرہ تو اس کو لا بس شمار نہیں کیا جائے گا بلکہ حامل شمار کریں گے، اور اس پر ضمان واجب ہوگا کیونکہ ان چیزوں کو پہننے کی کوئی حاجت نہیں ہے۔
And the difference is that the carrier of an object intends its preservation, so there is no difficulty in restricting it with the condition of safety. The wearer does not intend to preserve what he wears, so restricting it with the condition we mentioned would cause hardship. Hence we made it absolutely permissible. And from Muhammad: when one wears something not normally worn, he is like the carrier, because need does not call for wearing it. Translation: And the difference is that a person carrying an object intends its preservation, so there is no objection to qualifying it with the condition of safety. But the wearer does not intend preservation of the garment he wears; thus qualifying it with the condition we mentioned would cause hardship. So we deemed it absolutely permissible. It is narrated from Muhammad that when one wears something not normally worn (by custom), he is like the carrier, because need does not call for wearing it. Commentary: Why was a difference made between carrying and wearing? Because in the first, liability is obligatory, in the second it is not. So now it is explained: the carrier intends preservation of the item; if here the condition of safety is imposed—that safety condition is necessary, otherwise liability is due—there is no difficulty. But the wearer's purpose is not preservation of the garment; so if the condition of safety were also imposed here, great hardship would follow. Therefore wearing is deemed absolutely permissible without any condition, so no liability is due on the wearer. It is narrated from Imam Muhammad: if one wears something not customarily worn, like a saddle-cloth or a donkey's load, then he is not considered a wearer but rather a carrier, and liability is due on him, because there is no need to wear such things.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage explains the legal distinction between a carrier (حامل) and a wearer (لابس) regarding liability (ضمان) for damage caused by the object. The author clarifies that a carrier intends to preserve the item, so it is reasonable to hold him to a condition of safety; if the item gets damaged due to negligence, he is liable. A wearer, on the other hand, does not intend preservation of the garment, so imposing such a condition would cause hardship; therefore wearing is considered absolutely permissible and no liability attaches. However, Imam Muhammad adds an exception: if one wears something that is not normally worn (like a saddle or load), then the action is akin to carrying, and liability is due because there is no genuine need. This ruling is part of the chapter on diyat (blood money and injuries) and demonstrates Hanafi reliance on custom (urf) and intent (niyyah) in determining liability.

Key Terms
حامل (ḥāmil): A person who carries an object, intending its preservation, and thus is liable if the object causes harm due to negligence.
لابس (lābis): A person who wears something, not intending its preservation, and thus is not liable for harm caused by the worn item.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for damage or loss caused by one's action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person wears a garment and it falls and injures someone, is he liable?
A) Yes, because he caused the harm.
B) No, because he did not intend preservation; wearing is absolutely permissible.
C) Yes, because all actions causing harm incur liability.
D) No, unless the garment is not normally worn, then he is liable as a carrier.

Key Takeaway
Liability for damage depends on the actor's intent and custom: carriers are liable for preserving items, wearers are not liable for customary attire, with an exception for unusual items per Imam Muhammad.

Liability for Placing Items in a Mosque
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for items placed in a mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding liability for harm caused by placing items in a mosque, and distinguish between the two rulings based on authority over mosque management.

Core Idea
Liability for harm in a mosque depends on the doer's relationship to the mosque: members are immune, outsiders may be liable.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a lamp or mat placed in a clan mosque causes someone's death. Who pays blood money? Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagreed on the answer. The ruling turns on whether the action is considered an absolute act of worship or a management decision subject to safety. Understanding this dispute illuminates how Hanafi jurists balance religious freedom and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the case: a mosque belonging to a clan; a member or outsider places items (lamp, mats, pebbles); someone dies from the item falling. The legal question: Is the placer liable for compensation?
The scenario is specific to a clan mosque, not a public mosque.
2
State the Two Positions
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a clan member is not liable, but an outsider is liable. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that neither is liable because the actions are acts of worship.
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's Proof
Sahibayn argue that placing items for the mosque are acts of worship (qurbāt) and everyone is permitted to perform them without a condition of safety, analogizing to doing so with permission.
Their proof is based on the universal permissibility of worship acts.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's Response
Abu Hanifa distinguishes: mosque management (appointing imam, opening doors) belongs to the clan. Their actions are absolutely permissible. Outsiders' actions are either transgression (if unauthorized) or permissible but conditioned on safety, so liability attaches if harm occurs.
The key is authority over mosque affairs.
5
Identify the Practical Consequence
The difference matters in a court: if an outsider's lamp kills, Abu Hanifa would order blood money; Sahibayn would not. This shows how a legal principle affects real liability.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وإذا كان المسجد للعشيرة فعلق رجل منهم فيه قنديلا أو جعل فيه بوارى أو حصاه فعطب به رجل لم يضمن وإن كان الذي فعل ذالك من غير العشيرة ضمن قالوا هذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا يضمن في الوجهين لأن هذه من القرب وكل أحد مأذون في إقامتها فلايتقيد بشرط السلامة كما إذا فعله بإذن واحد من أهل المسجد
ترجمہ محمد نے فرمایا اور جب کہ مسجد کسی قوم کی ہو پس ان میں سے کسی شخص سے قندیل نکالی یا اس میں بورے ڈالے یا اس میں کنکر الی پس اس کی وجہ سے کوئی شخص ہلاک ہو گیا تو وہ ضامن نہ ہوگا اور اگر وہ شخص جس نے یہ کام کیا ہے ان لوگوں کا غیر ہوتو وہ ضامن ہوگا، مشائخ نے فرمایا کہ یہ ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ دونوں صورتوں میں ضامن نہ ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ یہ افعال قربات ہیں ، اور قربات کی ادائیگی میں ہر شخص کو اجازت ہے پس یہ سلامتی کی شرط کے ساتھ مقید نہ ہوگا جیسا کہ اس کو اس نے (جو عشیرہ کا غیر ہے ) اہل مسجد میں سے کسی کی اجازت سے کیا ہو۔
تشریح ایک مسجد ہے جو کسی قوم کی مخصوص ہے وہی اس کے بانی ہیں اور متولی ہیں اور کسی نے اس مسجد میں بوریئے ڈالے یا قندیل یا جھاڑ فانوس لٹکا یا وغیرہ پھر کوئی ان کے گرنے میں دب کر مر گیا تو اس پر ضمان ہوگا یا نہیں ؟
تو اس میں تفصیل ہے اگر ان امور کو انجام دینے والا شخص اس قوم میں سے ہے جن کی مسجد ہے تو پھر بالا تفاق ضمان نہیں ہے۔ اور اگر کوئی اور شخص ہے تو اس میں امام صاحب کے نزدیک اس شخص پر ضمان ہوگا، اور صاحبین کے نزدیک ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ لیکن اگر اس نے اہل مسجد کی اجازت سے یہ کام کئے ہوں تو پھر امام صاحب کے نزدیک بھی وہ ضامن نہ ہو گا۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ سارے کام قربت ہیں تو اب کے کام ہیں اور کار ثواب کرنے کی سب کو اجازت ہوتی ہے اور قربات کی ادائیگی میں سلامتی کی شرط نہیں ہوا کرتی لہذا اگر وہ ان کی اجازت سے یہ کام کرتا تب بھی تو اس پر ضمان نہیں ہے۔
تنبیه او حصاہ، یا کنکر ڈال دی ہوں یہ اس زمانہ کی بات ہے جب مسجدوں میں فرش نہیں ہوتا تھا اور کنکر ڈالنے کی ضرورت پیش آتی میں یا کنکر یہ کی فرش تھا اور کی ب کنکر ڈالنا مسجد کی تعظیم کے خلاف شمار ہوگا۔
ولأبي حنيفة وهو الفرق أن التدبير فيما يتعلق بالمسجد لأهله دون غيرهم كنصب الإمام واختيار المتولى وفتح بابه وإغلاقه وتكرار الجماعة إذا سبقهم بها غير أهله فكان فعلهم مباحا مطلقا غير مقيد بشرط السلامة وفعل غيرهم تعديا أو مباحا مقيدا بشرط السلامة
ترجمہ اور ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل اور یہی وجہ فرق ہے یہ ہے کہ انتظام اس سلسلہ میں جو مسجد سے متعلق ہے اس کے اہل کے لئے ہے نہ کہ ان کے غیر کے لئے جیسے امام کو مقرر کرنا اور متولی کو اختیار کرنا اور اس کا دروازہ کھولنا اور اس کو بند کرنا اور جماعت کا تکرار جب کہ ان کا غیران سے پہلے جماعت کرے تو ان کا فعل مطلقاً مباح ہوگا جو سلامتی کی شرط سے مقید نہ ہوگا اور ان کے غیر کا فعل تعدی ہوگا یا ایسا مبارح ہوگا جو سلامتی کی شرط کے ساتھ مقید ہوگا۔
تشریح یہ امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل ہے اور اس دلیل سے عشیرہ اور ان کے غیر کے درمیان بھی امتیاز ہو جائے گا، فرماتے ہیں کہ مسجد کے امور کا انتظام وہ اہل مسجد کا کام ہے۔ دوسروں کا کام نہیں ہے لہذا ان کا فعل مطلقاً مباح ہے جس میں سلامتی کی شرط نہیں ہے اور دوسروں کا فعل یا تو تعدی ہوگا یا مباح ہوگا اور جب مباح ہوگا تو سلامتی سے مقید ہو گا لہذا نوات اسلامی کی وجہ سے ضمان واجب ہو گا دلیل نام ہوگئی۔
الہذا مسجد میں امام کا تقر ر اور اس کا عزل اور متولی کا انتخاب نیز مسجد کا دروازہ کھولنا اور بند کرنا یہ اہل مسجد کا کام ہے۔ نیز اگر دوسرے لوگوں نے اہل محلہ سے پہلے مسجد میں جماعت کر لی تو اہل محلہ بلا کراہت دوبارہ جماعت کریں گے ورنہ جماعت ثانیہ بقول محقق مکروہ تحریمی ہے۔
Muhammad said: When the mosque belongs to a clan, then if a man from among them hangs a lamp, or puts mats or pebbles, and a man dies because of it, he is not liable. But if the one who did that is not of the clan, he is liable. They (the scholars) said: this is according to Abu Hanifa. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is not liable in both cases, because these are acts of worship, and everyone is permitted to perform them, so they are not restricted by the condition of safety, just as if he did it with the permission of one of the mosque's members. Translation: Muhammad said: When the mosque belongs to a clan, then if a man from among them hangs a lamp, or puts mats or pebbles, and a man dies because of it, he is not liable. But if the doer is from outside the clan, he is liable. The scholars said: this is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Sahibayn) said: he is not liable in either case, because these actions are acts of worship, and everyone is permitted to perform them, so they are not restricted by a condition of safety, just as if he did it with permission from one of the mosque's members. Commentary: There is a mosque specific to a certain community; they are its founders and trustees. A person places mats, hangs a lamp or chandelier, etc., and someone gets crushed and dies—is there liability? Detail: If the doer is from the community, then by agreement no liability. If he is an outsider, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa there is liability; according to Sahibayn no liability. But if he did it with permission from the mosque's people, then even Imam Abu Hanifa says no liability. The proof of Sahibayn: all these are acts of worship; everyone is permitted to do good deeds, and acts of worship are not conditioned on safety. Note: Pebbles were put down in the past when mosques had no flooring; but nowadays it might be against reverence. And for Abu Hanifa, the distinction is that management of mosque affairs is for its people, not others—like appointing imam, selecting trustee, opening and closing doors, and repeating congregation if outsiders preceded them. Thus their action is absolutely permissible not conditioned on safety, while the outsider's action is either transgression or permissible conditioned on safety. Commentary: This is Imam Abu Hanifa's proof, distinguishing between the clan and others. The management of mosque matters is the job of the mosque's people. Others' actions are either transgression or permissible but conditioned on safety, so liability applies due to negligence. Therefore, appointment of imam, removal, selection of trustee, opening and closing doors, etc., are the rights of the mosque people. Also, if outsiders perform congregation before them, the residents can repeat without dislike; otherwise, second congregation is makruh tahrimi according to the verifying scholars.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a mosque belongs to a particular clan (عشیرہ). If a member of that clan hangs a lamp, spreads mats, or puts pebbles in the mosque, and someone is injured or dies as a result, the member is not liable. If an outsider does the same, Imam Abu Hanifa holds him liable, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold him not liable because these are acts of worship (قربات) that anyone may perform without condition of safety. However, if the outsider acts with explicit permission from a mosque member, even Abu Hanifa agrees that there is no liability. The key reasoning: management of the mosque belongs to its people (like appointment of imam, opening/closing doors), so their actions are absolutely permissible. Outsiders' actions are either transgression (if unauthorized) or permissible but conditioned on safety, making them liable if harm occurs. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that authority over mosque affairs is limited to those with rightful connection, and that liability can depend on one's relationship to the property.

Key Terms
عشیرة (ʿashīrah): A clan or close-knit group that owns or manages a particular mosque.
قربات (qurbāt): Acts of worship or pious deeds that bring one closer to Allah.
تعدی (taʿaddī): Transgression or overstepping one's bounds; an action done without authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if an outsider hangs a lamp in a clan's mosque without permission and someone dies, is the outsider liable?
A) No, because hanging a lamp is an act of worship.
B) Yes, because management is for the clan, so the outsider's action is transgression.
C) No, if it is an act of worship, it is absolutely permissible.
D) Yes, but only if the lamp was not securely fastened.

Key Takeaway
Liability for harm caused by items placed in a mosque in Hanafi law depends on the authority relationship: members of the mosque's clan are not liable, while outsiders may be liable if they act without authorization.

Liability for Placing Items in a Mosque by Non-Members
Intra-Hanafi dispute on daman for non-member's acts in mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding liability for acts of devotion causing harm in a mosque, and apply the principle that the proper method (seeking permission) affects liability.

Core Idea
A non-member's act of devotion in a mosque does not automatically exempt from liability; without permission, the act is conditional on safety.

This lesson explores an important intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a person who places items in a mosque for devotional purposes can be held liable if those items cause injury or death. The Sahibayn argue that since the act is qurbat (devotion), it is absolutely permissible and no liability attaches. Imam Abu Hanifa, however, distinguishes between the mosque's own people and outsiders, holding that outsiders must ensure safety or obtain permission. This distinction reveals a deeper principle: even good deeds must be performed in the correct manner, or liability may arise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
What did the person do (placed mats, hung lamps), and what is the point of disagreement? The key is whether liability (daman) arises when the act causes death.
2
State the ruling of each party
State Abu Hanifa's position: liability unless the actor is from the mosque's people or has permission. State Sahibayn's position: no liability because the act is qurbat.
3
Analyze the proof of each
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof: management of mosque affairs is exclusive to its people, so others' acts are transgression or conditional on safety. Examine Sahibayn's proof: qurbat is unrestricted; safety is not a condition.
4
Understand the Hanafi response to Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa responds that intention of devotion does not negate liability if the proper method is missed, analogous to a single witness for adultery who becomes liable for qadhf.
The proper method here is seeking permission from the mosque's people.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a new case (e.g., a non-member hangs a fan without permission), determine liability according to each position, and state the mufta bihi.

Book of Blood-Money (Diyat). [Page 212] Ashraf al-Bidaya Sharh Urdu Hidaya Volume 15. ...and in the performance of acts of devotion (qurbat), every person is permitted, so this will not be restricted by a condition of safety, just as that which he (who is not of the tribe) did with the permission of someone from the people of the mosque. Explanation: There is a mosque that is specific to a certain tribe; they are its founders and trustees. If someone placed mats in this mosque, or hung a lamp or a chandelier, etc., and then someone was crushed and died under their fall, will there be liability (daman) or not? There is detail: if the person performing these acts is from that tribe (the owners of the mosque), then by consensus there is no liability. If he is another person, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa there is liability on that person, and according to the two companions (Sahibayn) liability is not obligatory. However, if he did these acts with the permission of the people of the mosque, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa also he will not be liable. The proof of the two companions is that all these acts are acts of devotion (qurbat), so they are good deeds, and everyone is permitted to do good deeds, and the performance of qurbat is not conditioned upon safety. Hence, even if he does these acts without their permission, there is no liability. Note: Pebbles or gravel—this is a matter of that time when mosques did not have flooring and the need to spread gravel arose... The proof of Abu Hanifa: And for Abu Hanifa, and this is the distinction, that management of matters related to the mosque is for its people, not for others—like appointing an imam, choosing a trustee, opening and closing its door, and repeating the congregation when someone else precedes them in prayer. Therefore, their act is absolutely permissible (mubah), not restricted by a condition of safety. The act of others is either transgression (ta'addi) or permissible but restricted by a condition of safety. Translation and explanation: This is Imam Abu Hanifa's proof, and by this proof the distinction between the tribe and others is established. He says that the management of mosque affairs is the job of the mosque's people, not others. Hence their act is absolutely permissible without safety condition, and others' act is either transgression or permissible restricted by safety, so due to... liability becomes obligatory. Therefore, appointing an imam, removing him, choosing a trustee, opening and closing the mosque door—these are the tasks of the mosque's people. Also, if other people pray in congregation before the local residents, the residents will pray again without dislike (karahah); otherwise, repeating the congregation is, according to the verifiers, prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi). Response to the two companions' proof: 'And intention of devotion does not negate liability if he errs in the method, just as when one alone testifies to adultery, or the method in our case is seeking permission from the people of the mosque.' Translation: Intention of devotion is not contrary to liability when he misses the correct method, like in the testimony of adultery... And the method in this issue is to seek permission from the people of the mosque. Explanation: The two companions said that when his act is devotion, there will be no liability. The response is that if in devotion the correct method is abandoned, then there is liability—i.e., devotion and liability are not contradictory. For example, testifying to adultery is devotion for the purpose of protecting the rights of Allah, but the condition for acceptance of testimony is four witnesses. Therefore, if one person gives testimony of adultery, then instead of testimony it becomes slander (qadhf) and the witness becomes liable for the hadd penalty for slander, but the act in itself is devotion. Similarly, in the mentioned case, the method of performing the devotion was to seek permission from the local residents, but he erred, so liability becomes obligatory. Note: The devotion in solitary testimony is questionable.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability (daman) for causing death through acts in a mosque that are normally considered meritorious (qurbat). The issue: if a non-member of the mosque’s owning tribe places mats or hangs lamps and someone dies from their fall, is he liable? The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that since these are acts of devotion (qurbat), they are permissible for anyone, and no condition of safety is attached, hence no liability. Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the mosque’s own people and others: the management of mosque affairs belongs to the people of the mosque; their acts are absolutely permissible. Others’ acts are either transgression or permissible but conditional on safety. If they fail to ensure safety, they are liable. The text then provides Abu Hanifa’s proof and a response to the Sahibayn: the intention of devotion does not preclude liability if one errs in the proper method—just as a single witness for adultery intends devotion but becomes liable for qadhf. The proper method here is seeking permission from the mosque’s people. Thus, without permission, the non-member’s act is not absolute; it is conditional on safety, and if harm occurs, he is liable.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Legal liability to compensate for harm, specifically payment of diyah (blood-money) for causing death.
قربت (qurbat): Acts of devotion or worship that draw one closer to Allah, such as acts of charity or service to the mosque.
تعدی (ta'addi): Transgression or wrongful act exceeding legal bounds, which incurs liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when is a non-member of the mosque's tribe liable for placing a lamp that falls and kills someone?
A) In all cases, because non-members cannot perform any act in the mosque.
B) Only if he did not obtain permission from the mosque's people; if he had permission, he is not liable.
C) Never, because hanging a lamp is a qurbat.
D) Only if he was sitting in the mosque at the time.

Key Takeaway
A non-member of a mosque who places items for devotion can be held liable for resulting harm if he did not obtain permission from the mosque's people, because the act is not absolutely permissible; it is conditional on safety.

Liability for a Person Sitting in the Mosque Causing Someone's Death
Intra-Hanafi dispute on daman for mosque sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on liability for a person sitting in a mosque when someone dies by tripping over him, distinguish between prayer and non-prayer scenarios, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Sitting in prayer in the mosque exempts from diyah; without prayer, Abu Hanifa holds liability, Sahibayn do not.

This passage from Al-Hidayah examines a specific case of accidental death in the mosque: a person is sitting and a passerby trips over him and dies. Is the sitter liable for diyah? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement. The ruling hinges on whether the sitting was for prayer or for a non-prayer activity. Let's dissect the arguments and see how the distinction is drawn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base case
The passage starts with the general case: a man from the mosque community sits and someone dies by tripping. The initial ruling depends on whether the sitter is in prayer or not.
The text uses 'jahala' (he sat) indicating deliberate sitting.
2
Note the consensus for prayer
If the person is in prayer, there is consensus (ittifaq) that he is not liable. Prayer is a pure act of devotion; sitting is mandatory for it.
This is undisputed among the Hanafis.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's position
If not in prayer, Abu Hanifa holds the sitter liable. He views non-prayer sitting as an avoidable act that may cause harm.
Reasoning: The sitter could have chosen a safer spot or position.
4
Understand the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the sitter is not liable in any case, as sitting for permissible or meritorious acts (like recitation or teaching) is not a transgression.
They consider the act itself not harmful per se.
5
Extend to other scenarios
The passage lists seven situations (e.g., sitting for Quran, teaching, waiting for prayer, sleeping, passing through, conversation) and states they follow the same difference. For i'tikaf, there are two opinions: one maintains the dispute, another says no liability by consensus.
This shows the dispute applies broadly to all non-prayer seating in the mosque.

He (Muhammad) said: And if a man from among them (the mosque's people) sits in it, and a man is injured (dies) because of him, then if he was in prayer, he is not liable; if he was not in prayer, he is liable. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Sahibayn) said: He is not liable in any case. As for the mu'takif, it is said that it is on this difference, and it is said that by consensus he is not liable. Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents another intra-Hanafi dispute about liability (daman) when a person from the mosque's community (ahl al-masjid) is sitting in the mosque and someone trips over him and dies. The ruling distinguishes between being in prayer and not in prayer. According to Imam Muhammad's narration (as part of Sahibayn), if the sitting person is praying, there is consensus (ittifaq) that he is not liable, because prayer is a pure act of devotion and his sitting is necessary for the prayer. If he is not praying, then Abu Hanifa holds him liable because his sitting is not an act of devotion per se, and it may be considered a form of causing harm through one's presence without necessity. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that he is not liable in any case, as sitting in the mosque, even without prayer, is a permissible or meritorious act (e.g., for Quran recitation, teaching, etc.) and is not considered transgression. The text then applies the same difference to various scenarios: sitting for Quran, teaching, waiting for prayer, sleeping during or outside prayer, passing through (though a passerby is not a sitter), and sitting for conversation. For i'tikaf (seclusion in the mosque), there are two opinions: one that it follows the same difference, and another that there is consensus of no liability because i'tikaf is a highly recommended act and the person is essentially engaged in devotion. The passage ends with listing seven situations by the author (al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah), but the OCR cuts off before completing them.

Key Terms
اعتکاف (i'tikaf): Seclusion in the mosque for worship, typically during the last ten days of Ramadan, considered a supererogatory act of devotion.
صاحبین (Sahibayn): The two companions of Imam Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
اختلاف (ikhtilaf): Difference of opinion among Hanafi jurists on a particular ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, in which situation is there consensus (ittifaq) that a sitting person is NOT liable if someone dies by tripping over him?
A) When he is sleeping in the mosque.
B) When he is sitting for teaching Quran.
C) When he is performing prayer.
D) When he is in i'tikaf.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, sitting in prayer in the mosque completely exempts from liability for accidental death, but non-prayer sitting is disputed between Abu Hanifa (liable) and the Sahibayn (not liable).

Qurbah Does Not Negate Gharamah When the Method is Erred
States and proofs the principle that intention of proximity does not negate liability when the method is wrong.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that an intention of proximity (qurbah) does not negate liability (gharamah) when the prescribed method is not followed, using the analogy of a single witness to zina.

Core Idea
Good intention does not excuse liability if you err in the correct method.

In Hanafi fiqh, the principle that qurbah (intention to draw near to Allah) does not automatically negate liability if the method is wrong is established through clear examples. This lesson explains the principle by addressing the objection of Sahibayn and proving it with the analogy of a single witness to zina.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Understand the claim: 'qurbah does not negate gharamah if the method is wrong.'
The Arabic: وقصد القربة لاينافى الغرامة إذا أخطأ الطريق
2
Identify the dispute
Recognize the objection of Sahibayn that qurbah negates liability entirely.
Sahibayn's view is presented and then refuted.
3
Analyze the proof by analogy
Examine the example of a single witness to zina: the act is qurbah to uphold Allah's rights, but the method (four witnesses) is not followed, resulting in qadhf and hadd.
This shows that method conditions liability.
4
Apply the method to the case at hand
In the diyat case, the correct method was seeking permission from the mosque people; erring in that method triggers liability.
The analogy directly applies.
5
Extract the ruling
Conclude that liability (gharamah) becomes wajib when the method is wrong, despite good intention.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحبین کی دلیل کا جواب
كتاب الديات
وقصد القربة لاينافى الغرامة إذا أخطأ الطريق كما إذا تفرد بالشهادة على الزنا أو الطريق فيما نحن فيه الاستيذان من أهله.
ترجمہ: اور ارادہ قربت غرامت کے منافی نہیں ہے جب کہ وہ طریقہ چوک جائے جیسا کہ زنا کی شہادت میں تفرد و اختیار کیا اور طریقہ اس مسئلہ میں جس میں ہم ہیں اہل مسجد سے اجازت لینا ہے۔ تشریح: صاحبین نے فرمایا تھا کہ جب اس کا فعل قربت ہو تو ضمان نہ ہو گا اس کا جواب دیا کہ اگر قربت میں اصل طریقہ چھوڑ دیا تو اس میں بھی تاوان ہو جاتا ہے یعنی قربت و تاوان میں منافات نہیں ہے۔ جیسے زناء کی شہادت دینا حقوق اللہ کی حفاظت کی غرض سے قربت ہے لیکن شرط قبول شہادت یہ ہے کہ گواہ چار ہوں لہذا اگر ایک شخص نے زنا کی گواہی دی تو اب بجائے گواہی کے یہ قذف ہوگا اور اس شاہد پر حد قذف واجب ہوگی لیکن شاہد کا فعل فی نفسہ قربت ہے۔ اسی طرح مذکورہ صورت میں قربت کی ادائیگی کا طریقہ یہ تھا کہ وہ اہل محلہ سے اجازت لیتا لیکن وہ چوک گیا تو ضمان واجب ہوگا۔ تنبیہ: تنہا شخص کی گواہی کا قربت ہونا محل تامل ہے۔
Response to the proof of the two companions. Book of Diyat. 'And intention of proximity does not negate liability if he errs in the method, just as when he alone gives testimony on zina, or the method in our case is seeking permission from its people.' Translation: And intention of nearness to Allah is not contrary to compensation when he mistakes the method, like what was chosen in the testimony of zina, and the method in this issue we are in is seeking permission from the people of the mosque. Explanation: The two companions had said that when his action is an act of nearness, there will be no liability. The answer was given that if in proximity the original method is abandoned, then in that also liability occurs, meaning there is no contradiction between proximity and liability. For example, giving testimony of zina is an act of nearness for the sake of protecting the rights of Allah, but the condition for accepting testimony is that the witnesses be four. So if one person testifies to zina, then it becomes qadhf instead of testimony, and the hadd of qadhf becomes obligatory on that witness, but the action of the witness in itself is proximity. Similarly, in the mentioned case, the method of performing the act of proximity was that he should seek permission from the people of the neighborhood, but he erred, so liability becomes wajib. Caution: That the testimony of a single person being a act of proximity is a point of consideration.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof by analogy. The text states the principle that an intention to draw near to Allah (qurbah) does not negate liability (gharamah) if the method of action is erroneous. The proof is the example of a single witness testifying to zina: giving testimony is an act of qurbah, but because the method (i.e., four witnesses) is not followed, the witness commits qadhf and becomes liable to hadd. Similarly, in the diyat case under discussion, the correct method was to seek permission from the people of the mosque; erring in that method results in liability despite good intention.

Key Terms
قربت (qurbah): An act intended to draw near to Allah, such as testimony to uphold divine rights.
غرامت (gharamah): Liability to pay compensation for harm caused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle stated in this passage, does a good intention (qurbah) negate liability (gharamah) if the method is wrong?
A) Yes, always.
B) No, it does not negate liability if the method is wrong.
C) Only if the action is for the sake of Allah.
D) Only if the method is agreed upon by the people of the mosque.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a good intention (qurbah) does not automatically exempt from liability when the prescribed method is not followed.

Liability of a Sitter in the Mosque: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on daman for sitting in mosque causing death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on liability for harm caused by sitting in the mosque and apply the dispute's practical consequences to various common scenarios.

Core Idea
Liability for harm in the mosque depends on whether the sitter is in prayer, and for Abu Hanifa, even the purpose of sitting matters.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on tort liability. When a person sitting in a mosque inadvertently causes another to fall and die, the ruling on liability (daman) varies depending on the sitter's activity. The matn states the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa (liability for non-prayer) and the Sahibayn (no liability in any lawful sitting). Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to see how the Hanafi school handles nuanced questions of causation, purpose, and legal analogy within the mosque's sacred space.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Read the matn carefully: if the sitter is in prayer, no liability by consensus. If not in prayer, Abu Hanifa says wajib (liable), Sahibayn say not liable. Why the difference?
The point of agreement is crucial: prayer itself always exempts.
2
List the seven non-prayer scenarios
The commentary enumerates: recitation, teaching, waiting for prayer, sleeping during prayer, sleeping outside prayer, passing through, and sitting for conversation. Each falls under the same ikhtilaf.
The waiting-for-prayer scenario is special: even Abu Hanifa exempts it, as the commentary explicitly notes.
3
Analyze the proofs
Sahibayn argue that the mosque is for prayer and dhikr; waiting is necessary for congregation, thus sitting is permissible (mubah) and must be exempt. They cite the hadith that the one waiting for prayer is in prayer by ruling (hukman). Abu Hanifa argues that the mosque was built specifically for prayer; other activities are merely attached, so a distinction must be drawn.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and its scope is debated.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, a student sitting to study is liable if someone dies; under Sahibayn, no liability. But the one waiting for prayer is exempt even by Abu Hanifa.
This nuance shows Abu Hanifa's focus on the original purpose (prayer) versus ancillary purposes.
5
Consider the mu'takif
The mu'takif (one in i'tikaf) has two reports: either the same dispute or consensus on no liability. This shows that even on a closer analogy to prayer, disagreement persists.
The later Hanafi authorities tend towards exemption for direct acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اہل مسجد میں سے کوئی شخص مسجد میں بیٹھا تھا کہ دوسرا ہلاک ہو گیا، بیٹھنے والے پر ضمان ہے یا نہیں خواہ بیٹھنے والا نماز میں ہو یا نہ ہو؟ اقوال فقہاء:
قال وإن جلس فيه رجل منهم فعطب به رجل لم يضمن إن كان فى الصلاة وإن كان في غير الصلاة ضمن وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا يضمن على كل حال ولو كان جالسا لقراءة القرآن أو للتعليم أو للصلاة أو نام فيه فى أثناء الصلاة أو نام في غير الصلاة أو مر فيه مار أو قعد فيه لحديث فهو على هذا الاختلاف وأما عتكف فقد قيل على هذا الاختلاف وقيل لا يضمن بالاتفاق ترجمہ: محمد نے فرمایا اور اگر مسجد میں بیٹھا ان میں سے (اہل مسجد میں سے) پس اس کی وجہ سے کوئی شخص ہلاک ہو گیا تو اگر وہ نماز میں ہو تو وہ ضامن نہ ہوگا اور اگر نماز میں نہ ہو تو وہ ضامن ہوگا اور یہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ ہر حال میں ضامن نہ ہوگا اور اگر قرات قرآن یا تعلیم کے لئے یا نماز کے لئے (انتظار میں) بیٹھا ہے یا نماز کے دوران مسجد میں سو گیا یا غیر صلوٰۃ میں مسجد میں سو گیا یا کوئی گزرنے والا مسجد میں سے گزرا یا بات چیت کے لئے مسجد میں بیٹھ گیا پس وہ اسی اختلاف پر ہے۔ اور بہرحال معتکف: پس کہا گیا ہے کہ اس اختلاف پر ہے اور کہا گیا ہے کہ بالاتفاق ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ تشریح: اہل مسجد میں سے کوئی شخص مسجد میں بیٹھا ہوا ہے اور اس سے کوئی شخص پھسل کر مر گیا تو ضمان ہوگا یا نہیں؟ تو فرمایا کہ اگر وہ شخص نماز میں ہے تو بالاتفاق ضامن نہیں ہے اور اگر نماز میں نہ ہو تو اس میں اختلاف ہے امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک ضمان واجب ہوگا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب وہ نماز میں نہ ہو تو اس کی سات صورتیں مصنف نے بیان فرمائی ہیں: (1) تلاوت قرآن کے لئے بیٹھا ہو (2) فقہ یا حدیث وغیرہ کے لئے بیٹھا ہو (3) نماز کے انتظار میں بیٹھا ہو (4) نماز پڑھ رہا تھا کہ نماز میں ہی سو گیا (5) نماز سے باہر سو گیا (6) مسجد میں ہو کر گزر رہا تھا کہ کوئی اس کی وجہ سے ہلاک ہو گیا (7) بات چیت کے لئے مسجد میں بیٹھ گیا ہو۔ اور اگر معتکف بیٹھا ہوا ہے اور اس سے ٹکرا کر کوئی مر جائے تو اس میں دو قول ہیں: یہ بھی مسئلہ اختلافی ہے، یا اجماعی ہے اس میں ضمان نہ ہوگا۔ تنبیہ: نماز کے انتظار میں بیٹھنے والا ضامن نہ ہوگا، امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک بھی۔ (شامی ص ۳۸۲ ج ۵)

لهما أن المسجد إنما بنى للصلاة والذكر ولا يمكنه أداء الصلاة بالجماعة إلا بانتظارها فكان الجلوس فيه مباحا لأنه من ضرورات الصلاة أو لأن المنتظر للصلاة في الصلاة حكما بالحديث فلايضمن كما إذا كان في الصلاة
