Who Can Be Addressed to Demolish a Leaning Wall?
General rule with illustrative list

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which persons can be lawfully addressed to demolish a leaning wall according to Hanafi law, and apply the principle that the addressee must have the ability to demolish.

Core Idea
A legal demand to demolish is valid only if the addressee can actually demolish.

In Hanafi property law, a dangerous leaning wall or an airspace encroachment creates a right to demand its removal. However, not everyone can be forced to comply. This lesson unpacks the foundational rule (qā'idah kulliyyah) that the demand (takhāṭub) must be directed to someone with the legal and practical ability to demolish. We will see why a mortgagee cannot be addressed, while a father of a minor can.

Guided Path
1
State the General Principle
Read the opening sentence: addressing (takhāṭub) is valid only towards one who is able to demolish. This is the core condition.
2
Identify Persons Who CANNOT Be Addressed
The text explicitly lists: al-murtaḥin (mortgagee), al-musta'jir (lessee), al-mūda' (depositee), sākin al-dār (resident). Ask yourself: why does each lack ability?
All lack ownership or control over the wall.
3
Identify Persons Who CAN Be Addressed
The list: al-rāhin (pledgor), al-waṣī (executor), father or mother of a minor, al-mukātab (slave under contract), and the trading slave (al-`abd al-tājir). For each, note the basis: ability through redemption (fakāk), guardianship (wilāyah), or legal capacity.
Each of these persons either owns the wall, can gain control, or acts on behalf of the owner.
4
Understand the Reasoning for Each Category
For the pledgor: he can redeem (fakāk) the pledged property, thereby regaining control. For the executor and parents: they have wilāyah (guardianship) over the minor's property. For the mukātab and trading slave: they have independent legal capacity regarding their property.
The rule ensures the demand is not futile or unjust.
5
Apply the Rule to New Scenarios
Given a new person (e.g., a guardian appointed by the court, a partner in a partnership), determine whether they have the ability to demolish. Use the principles of ownership, control, and guardianship.
The key test: can this person physically and legally access the wall and demolish it?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدہ کلیہ
والأصل أنه يصح التقدم إلى كل من يتمكن من نقض الحائط وتفريغ الهواء ومن لا يتمكن منه لا يصح العقدم إليه كالمرتهن والمستأجر والمودع وساكن الدار ويصح التقدم إلى الراهن لقدرته على ذالك بواسطة الفكاك وإلى الوصى وإلى أب اليتيم أو أمه فى حائط الصبى لقيام الولاية وذكر الأم في الزيادات والضمان فى مال اليتيم لأن فعل هؤلاء كفعله وإلى المكاتب لأن الولاية له وإلى العبد التاجر سواء كار عليه دين أو لم يكن لأن ولاية النقض له
ترجمہ اور قاعدہ کلیہ یہ ہے کہ نقدم ہر اس شخص کی طرف صحیح ہے جو دیوار توڑنے پر اور فضاء کو خالی کرنے پر قادر ہو اور جو اس پر قادر نہ ؟ تو اس کی طرف تقدم صحیح نہیں ہے جیسے مرتہن اور مستاجر اور مودع اور گھر میں رہنے والا ۔ اور راہن کی طرف تقدم صحیح ہے اس کے قادر ہو۔
General rule: And the principle is that addressing (takhāṭub) is valid towards everyone who is able to demolish the wall and clear the airspace. And towards one who is not able, addressing is not valid, such as the mortgagee, lessee, depositee, and resident of the house. And addressing is valid towards the pledgor, because he has the ability to do that through redemption (fakāk); and towards the executor (waṣī), and towards the father of an orphan or his mother regarding the wall of the minor, because they have guardianship (wilāyah), and the mother is mentioned in al-Ziyādāt, and liability is on the orphan's property because their action is like his action; and towards the mukātab (slave under contract of manumission) because he has guardianship; and towards the trading slave, whether he has debt or not, because he has the authority to demolish. Translation: And the general rule is that addressing is valid towards every person who is able to demolish the wall and clear the airspace, and one who is not able, addressing him is not valid, like the mortgagee, lessee, depositee, and the resident of the house. And addressing is valid towards the pledgor, because he is able to do that through redemption.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage lays down a foundational rule (qā'idah kulliyyah) in Hanafi tort law regarding who can be formally called upon to remove a nuisance (a leaning wall or an encroachment on airspace). The principle is that such a legal call (takhāṭub) is valid only when the person addressed has the immediate ability to carry out the demolition. Thus, a mortgagee (murtaḥin), a lessee (musta'jir), a depositee (mūda'), or a mere resident (sākin al-dār) cannot be addressed because they lack ownership or control over the structure. Conversely, the following persons can be addressed: (1) the pledgor (rāhin) because he can redeem the property; (2) the executor (waṣī) of a minor; (3) the father or mother of a minor (the mother is specifically mentioned in the Ziyādāt, with the liability falling on the minor's property because their actions are like his); (4) the mukātab (a slave under contract of manumission) because he has a form of legal capacity; and (5) a trading slave (whether indebted or not) because he has the authority to demolish. The rule ensures that the legal demand is made only on someone who can actually comply, preventing futile or unjust demands.

Key Terms
التقدم (al-takhāṭub): The legal act of addressing or calling upon someone to demolish a leaning wall or clear an airspace encroachment.
ولایت (wilāyah): Legal authority or guardianship; here, the authority to act on behalf of a minor or incapacitated person, enabling a valid address.
فکاک (fakāk): Redemption; the ability of a pledgor to redeem pledged property to gain control over it, thus enabling him to demolish the wall.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The trading slave

Key Takeaway
A legal demand to demolish a leaning wall is valid only if the addressee has the practical and legal ability to carry out the demolition—ownership, guardianship, or a right to control the property.

Liability for Wall Collapse After Sale: The Role of Ability (Tamakkun)
Ruling on liability of seller and buyer when a wall is sold after demand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of tamakkun (legal ability) to determine liability when a dangerous structure is sold after a formal demand to demolish.

Core Idea
Liability for a dangerous wall follows ability to demolish — sale transfers ability, so liability shifts only if the buyer is also demanded.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from Al-Hidayah on tort liability when a leaning wall is sold after the owner has been formally demanded to demolish it. The key distinction is whether the tort arises from a continuing failure to act (leaving the wall standing while having ability) or from a positive act (placing a projection). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly assigning liability in property damage cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage carefully to identify the two main cases: (1) seller sells after being demanded, and (2) buyer is demanded after purchase. Also note the contrasting case of a projection.
The passage uses the example of a leaning wall vs. a skylight projection.
2
Track the element of tamakkun (ability)
For each scenario, determine whether the person had the legal ability to demolish at the time of the collapse. The ruling hinges on whether tamakkun existed or was removed.
Ability is lost when ownership transfers, since only the owner can demolish.
3
Apply the principle to the seller
The seller is not liable after sale because his ability to demolish ceased when he transferred ownership. The tort was based on 'leaving with ability'; once ability is gone, liability ends.
This is explicit in the text: 'because his ability ceased by the sale.'
4
Apply the principle to the buyer
The buyer is not liable unless he is formally demanded after purchase. If demanded and he fails to demolish while having ability, he becomes liable.
The demand (ishhad) creates a new obligation on the buyer.
5
Distinguish the projection case
For a projection (like a skylight), the tort is the act of placement itself, not a failure to remove. Sale does not annul that act, so the original tortfeasor remains liable even after selling.
This contrast illustrates that the 'illa (legal cause) differs between omission and commission.

Source Text

And if he sold the house after he served notice upon him, and the buyer took possession, he is free from liability because the tort is by leaving demolition while having ability, and his ability ceased by the sale, unlike extending a projection because he was a tortfeasor by placement and it is not annulled by sale, so he does not become free, as we mentioned. And there is no liability on the buyer because he was not served notice. And if he served notice upon him after his purchase, then he is liable for leaving the vacation while having ability after he was demanded. Translation: And if he sold the house after serving notice and the buyer took possession, then he becomes free from liability because the tort is by leaving demolition with ability, and by sale his ability is removed, unlike extending a skylight, because the owner is a tortfeasor by placement, and placement is not annulled by sale, so he does not become free, according to the detail we mentioned. And there is no liability on the buyer because notice was not served. And if notice was served on the buyer after his purchase, then he is liable for leaving demolition while having ability, after he was demanded. Commentary: Khalid's wall was leaning and he was demanded to demolish it, but he sold his house to Bakr. Now Khalid is not liable because he is no longer owner and has no right to demolish. Bakr is also not liable because he was not demanded. However, if after purchase he was demanded to demolish and he did not, then he becomes liable because he had ability and was demanded. But if someone extended a skylight, since he was a tortfeasor from the start, he does not become free from liability by selling the house; rather, liability remains on him.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on liability for damages caused by a leaning wall after the property is sold. The author first states the Arabic matn from Al-Hidayah, then provides an Urdu translation and commentary. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) The original owner who was demanded to demolish sells the house: he becomes free from liability because he no longer has the ability to demolish (since he is no longer owner). (2) The buyer is not liable unless he is also demanded after purchase. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the tort is based on leaving demolition while having ability; sale removes that ability. This is contrasted with a projection (such as a skylight) that is already placed; the placement itself is the tort, and sale does not annul it, so the original tortfeasor remains liable. The commentary uses the example of Khalid and Bakr to illustrate. This ruling highlights the Hanafi emphasis on ability (tamakkun) as the basis for liability in such torts.

Key Terms
أشهد عليه (ashhada 'alayhi): To serve formal legal notice or demand upon someone, typically with witnesses, to warn them of liability.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for damages or loss, often in the context of torts (jinayat).
تمكن (tamakkun): Legal ability or power to perform an action, such as demolishing a wall.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a buyer become liable for damages from a leaning wall after purchasing the house?
A) Immediately upon purchase
B) Only if he was formally demanded to demolish after purchase and failed to do so
C) Only if the wall was already leaning at the time of sale
D) He is never liable; liability stays with the seller

Key Takeaway
Liability for a dangerous wall follows the person who has both the ability to demolish and has been formally demanded to do so; sale transfers ability, shifting potential liability only after a new demand on the buyer.

Diyat — Blood Money
beginner

General Principle: Who Can Be Demanded to Demolish a Wall
General principle for valid demand to demolish

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which persons can be validly demanded to demolish a dangerous wall and state the Hanafi principle regarding capacity and authority.

Core Idea
Only those with both ability and legal authority can be demanded to demolish a wall.

This lesson presents a fundamental Hanafi legal principle (qaa'idah kulliyyah) used to determine whose demand to demolish a dangerous wall is legally valid. The principle hinges on two key criteria: physical ability (tamakkun) and legal authority (wilayah). By mastering this rule, you will be able to navigate similar cases involving liability for dangerous property.

Guided Path
1
State the general principle
Read the Arabic matn: 'والأصل أنه يصح التقدم إلى كل من يتمكن من نقض الحائط وتفريغ الهواء' — identify that validity depends on ability to demolish and vacate the airspace.
2
Identify those who cannot be demanded
List the four persons: mortgagee (المرتهن), lessee (المستأجر), depositary (المودع), and house dweller (ساكن الدار). Understand why each lacks ability or authority: they do not own the wall or have a right to demolish it.
These are the mu'ajjizaat (incapacitating factors).
3
Identify those who can be demanded
List the five persons: pledgor (الراهن), executor (الوصي), orphan's grandfather (أب اليتيم), orphan's mother (أمه), mukatab (المكاتب), and merchant slave (العبد التاجر). For each, note the reason: they have either the ability (via redemption) or guardianship (wilayah) over the wall.
The mother's inclusion is from Imam Muhammad's al-Ziyadat.
4
Understand the subsidiary ruling on liability
If the guardians were demanded but failed to demolish and the wall falls and kills someone, the liability is on the orphan's property, not on the guardians personally. Reason: their act is considered as the orphan's act.
This reinforces that the demand is against the person with authority, and liability follows the property.
5
Memorize the two categories
Create a mental table of who can be demanded and who cannot, with the underlying reasons. This principle is a memorization anchor for understanding capacity in nuisance and liability cases.
Focus on the criterion: ability + authority.

خود بچہ کے فعل کے مثل ہے۔
The principle is that addressing is valid to everyone who is able to demolish the wall and vacate the airspace, and whoever is not able, addressing him is not valid, like the mortgagee, the lessee, the depositary, and the inhabitant of the house. And addressing is valid to the pledgor due to his ability through redemption; and to the executor; and to the orphan's grandfather or his mother regarding the child's wall due to the existence of guardianship, and the mention of the mother is in al-Ziyadat, and the liability is on the orphan's property because their act is like his act; and to the mukatab because guardianship is for him; and to the merchant slave, whether he has debt or not, because the authority to demolish is for him. Translation: And the general principle is that addressing is valid to every person who is able to demolish the wall and vacate the airspace, and whoever is not able, addressing him is not valid, like the mortgagee, lessee, depositary, and house dweller. And addressing is valid to the pledgor because he is able (due to redemption); and to the executor; and to the orphan's grandfather or his mother regarding the child's wall due to establishment of guardianship, and the mention of the mother is in al-Ziyadat, and liability is on the orphan's property because their act is like his act; and addressing is valid to the mukatab because the authority to demolish is for him. Commentary: Here the author states a general principle that whoever is able to demolish the wall, demanding him is valid; whoever is not able, demanding him is not valid. Then he mentions some who are not able: mortgagee, lessee, depositary, and house dweller without ownership. Those to whom demand is valid include: pledgor (can redeem), executor, orphan's grandfather (here father's father is meant, else if father exists, not orphan), orphan's mother (mentioned by Imam Muhammad in al-Ziyadat), mukatab, and merchant slave (whether in debt or not, because the slave has authority to demolish). Question: If a child's wall falls after the guardians were demanded but did not demolish, and it kills someone, whose property is liable? Answer: The child's property, because the guardians' act is like the child's act.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents a general principle (qaa'idah kulliyyah) in Hanafi fiqh regarding the validity of addressing a demand to demolish a dangerous wall. The author first quotes the Arabic matn from Al-Hidayah, then provides Urdu translation and commentary. The principle states that a demand to demolish is only valid if the person addressed has both the physical ability and legal authority to demolish. Those who lack ability (e.g., mortgagee, tenant, depositary, mere inhabitant) cannot be validly demanded because they lack either ownership or authority. Those who can be demanded include the pledgor (who can redeem the property), the executor, the child's guardians (grandfather or mother), the mukatab (slave with contract of manumission), and the merchant slave (who has authority over his own affairs). The commentary also addresses a subsidiary issue: if a child's wall collapses after the guardians were demanded but failed to demolish, the liability falls on the child's property, not on the guardians personally, because their action is considered as the child's action. This principle illustrates the Hanafi school's systematic approach to liability, linking it to capacity (tamakkun) and authority (wilayah).

Key Terms
تقدم (taqaddum): Addressing someone with a formal legal demand, here specifically to demolish a wall.
نقض الحائط (naqd al-ha'it): Demolishing or pulling down a wall that poses a danger.
ولایت (wilayah): Legal authority or guardianship to act on behalf of another, especially regarding property.
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master to purchase his freedom through installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general principle, which of the following can be validly demanded to demolish a wall?
A) A mortgagee (murtahin)
B) A lessee (musta'jir)
C) An orphan's mother regarding the child's wall
D) A depositary (mudi')

Key Takeaway
The validity of a demand to demolish a dangerous wall depends on the person's ability and legal authority, not merely on ownership or possession.
